      1684 +/* The op category a `policy` decl serves, from `@policy(<cat
           +egory>)`: 1=bounds, 2=pool, 3=divide, 0=none.                
      1685 + * `policy` lexes as TOK_POLICY (a keyword), so the sequence 
           +is `@ policy ( <cat> )`. */                                  
      1686 +static int syntax_decl_policy_category(SyntaxView d) {       
      1687 +  if (!sv_present(d))                                        
      1688 +    return 0;                                                
      1689 +  int n = d.node->child_count;                               
      1690 +  for (int i = 0; i + 4 < n; i++) {                          
      1691 +    const SyntaxElem *at = &d.node->children[i];             
      1692 +    const SyntaxElem *kw = &d.node->children[i + 1];         
      1693 +    const SyntaxElem *lp = &d.node->children[i + 2];         
      1694 +    const SyntaxElem *cat = &d.node->children[i + 3];        
      1695 +    const SyntaxElem *rp = &d.node->children[i + 4];         
      1696 +    if (at->tag != SE_TOKEN || at->as.token.kind != TOK_AT ||
           + kw->tag != SE_TOKEN ||                                      
      1697 +        kw->as.token.kind != TOK_POLICY)                     
      1698 +      continue;                                              
      1699 +    if (lp->tag != SE_TOKEN || lp->as.token.kind != TOK_LPARE
           +N || cat->tag != SE_TOKEN ||                                 
      1700 +        cat->as.token.kind != TOK_IDENT || rp->tag != SE_TOKE
           +N || rp->as.token.kind != TOK_RPAREN)                        
      1701 +      continue;                                              
      1702 +    const char *p = d.src + cat->as.token.offset;            
      1703 +    size_t len = cat->as.token.length;                       
      1704 +    if (len == 6 && memcmp(p, "bounds", 6) == 0)             
      1705 +      return 1;                                              
      1706 +    if (len == 4 && memcmp(p, "pool", 4) == 0)               
      1707 +      return 2;                                              
      1708 +    if (len == 6 && memcmp(p, "divide", 6) == 0)             
      1709 +      return 3;                                              
      1710 +    return 0;                                                
      1711 +  }                                                          
      1712 +  return 0;                                                  
      1713 +}

      545 +/* As find_policy_sig, but matching a specific op category ‚ÄĒ `
          +abort`/`undefined` exist as both a bounds

          +cl `!name` resolves to. */                                    
      547 +static DeclSummary *find_policy_sig_cat(SemanticContext *ctx, 
          +const char *name, PolicyCategory cat) {                       
      548 +  if (!name)                                                  
      549 +    return NULL;                                              
      550 +  for (int i = 0; i < ctx->decl_count; i++) {                 
      551 +    DeclSummary *d = ctx->decls[i];                           
      552 +    if (d->is_policy && d->policy_category == cat && d->name &
          +& strcmp(d->name, name) == 0)                                 
      553 +      return d;                                               
      554 +  }                                                           
      555 +  return NULL;                                                
      556 +}

      558 +/* Validate an explicit `!name` against the op's category `wan
          +t` (and the func-total / `--no-abort` /                       
      559 + * `--no-undefined` rules). `d` is the enclosing decl; `loc` t
          +he op site. Emits at most one diagnostic. */                  
      560 +static void validate_explicit_policy(SemanticContext *ctx, Dec
          +lSummary *d, SourceLoc loc, const char *name,                 
      561 +                                     PolicyCategory want) {   
      562 +  /* Name-based rules that hold in any category: a func can ne
          +ver abort; flag enforcement. */                               
      563 +  if (strcmp(name, "abort") == 0) {                           
      564 +    if (d->kind == DECL_FUNC) {                               
      565 +      sem_emit_policy_func_aborts(ctx, loc, d->name ? d->name 
          +: "<anon>");                                                  
      566 +      return;                                                 
      567 +    }                                                         
      568 +    if (g_no_abort && decl_is_user_code(d)) {                 
      569 +      sem_emit_policy_abort_forbidden(ctx, loc, "this `!abort`
          +", "--no-abort");                                             
      570 +      return;                                                 
      571 +    }                                                         
      572 +  } else if (strcmp(name, "undefined") == 0 && g_no_undefined 
          +&& decl_is_user_code(d)) {                                    
      573 +    sem_emit_policy_undefined_forbidden(ctx, loc);            
      574 +    return;                                                   
      575 +  }

          +ust exist. */                                                 
      577 +  if (find_policy_sig_cat(ctx, name, want))                   
      578 +    return;                                                   
      579 +  DeclSummary *any = find_policy_sig(ctx, name);              
      580 +  if (!any)

      582 +  else                                                        
      583 +    sem_emit_policy_wrong_category(ctx, loc, name, policy_cat_
          +name(want), policy_cat_name(any->policy_category));

‚óŹ Root cause found ‚ÄĒ codegen.c:2287-2302. The &&/|| handler's comment says
  "arche expressions have no side effects and no traps, so eager evaluation is 
  indistinguishable from short-circuit." Policies invalidated that assumption:
  both operands are fully codegen'd (policy preludes emitted) before this point,
  then && just bitwise-combines the already-computed values. So a policy in
  either operand always runs.

  This is a soundness hole in the policy guarantee itself: a !abort in a
  short-circuited branch aborts at a site that's logically unreachable. The fix
  is real but moderately invasive ‚ÄĒ special-case OP_AND/OP_OR to branch and
  evaluate the RHS lazily in its own block (phi the result), instead of
  pre-computing right_val.

  The 3 tests are red and committed-as-red. Your call:

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ The short-circuit defect (a policy in a `&&`/`||` operand runs 
     unconditionally) is a real soundness hole ‚ÄĒ a `!abort` in a short-circuited
     branch aborts even though it's logically unreachable. Fix it now, or 
     defer? ‚Üí how does this work in other modern compilers? lets fix it now but 
     only if its a principled fix and not a workaround

‚óŹ This is the textbook case. Let me confirm the principled approach and then
  implement it.

  How modern compilers do it: Clang/LLVM, Rust, GCC all lower a && b to a
  branch, not a bitwise combine ‚ÄĒ the RHS is generated into its own basic block
  that's only entered when the LHS doesn't decide the result:

    %l = icmp ne <LHS>, 0
    br i1 %l, label %land.rhs, label %land.end   ; && : LHS false ‚Üí skip RHS
  land.rhs:
    %r = icmp ne <RHS>, 0                         ; RHS (and its side effects) 
  ONLY here
    br label %land.end
  land.end:
    %res = phi i1 [ false, %entry ], [ %r, %land.rhs ]

  (|| is the dual: br i1 %l, %lor.end, %lor.rhs, phi [true, entry].) That's
  exactly what clang's land.rhs/lor.rhs blocks are. It's the standard lowering ‚ÄĒ
  not a workaround. arche's bug is that it pre-evaluates both operands then
  bitwise-ands them, which only works when operands are effect-free.

  Let me look at the binary handler's structure to implement the branch
  lowering:

      2087  
      2088 +    /* Logical `&&` / `||` MUST short-circuit. With failure p
           +olicies an operand can abort (`_exit`) or                    
      2089 +     * mutate state, so the RHS may run ONLY when the LHS doe
           +sn't already decide the result ‚ÄĒ eager                       
      2090 +     * evaluation would fire a short-circuited-out `!abort`. 
           +Lower exactly like every modern compiler                     
      2091 +     * (clang's `land.rhs`/`lor.rhs`, Rust, GCC): branch on t

      2094 +    if (expr->data.binary.op == OP_AND || expr->data.binary.o
           +p == OP_OR) {

‚óŹ Everything is green. Here's what landed this session, all on top of the
  macro-policy model:

  Category check (your top priority)

  - @policy(bounds|divide) now flows through HIR (policy_category) ‚Üí lowering ‚Üí
  codegen. Policies resolve by name AND category, so abort and undefined exist
  as both a bounds and a divide policy and the op site picks which.
  - Wrong-category use is now a hard error instead of silently degrading to the
  raw op:
    - a / b !clamp ‚Üí "!clamp is a @policy(bounds) ‚ÄĒ this site needs a 
  @policy(divide) policy"
    - a[i] !zero ‚Üí the dual error
  - The old "intrinsic name" whitelist is gone ‚ÄĒ abort/undefined/zero/clamp/wrap
  are all ordinary categorized core decls. Only the genuinely name-based rules
  remain (abort forbidden in a func; --no-abort/--no-undefined).

  Abort writes to stderr (you asked for this)

  - abort now does dprintf(2, "arche: index out of bounds\n") / "arche: divide 
  by zero\n" then _exit(134). Used dprintf (not write) specifically so the core
  symbol can't collide with os.write in the all-stdlib codegen-test harness.

  Mid-expression control flow (you asked for tests, kept red, you called the 
  fix)

  - The tests exposed a real soundness hole: &&/|| evaluated both operands then
  bitwise-combined them ‚ÄĒ so a !abort in a short-circuited-out branch fired
  anyway, aborting at a logically-unreachable site.
  - You asked whether this is how modern compilers do it and to fix it only if
  principled. It is: the fix is the standard branch lowering (clang's
  land.rhs/lor.rhs, Rust, GCC) ‚ÄĒ branch on the LHS, evaluate the RHS (and its
  inlined policy prelude) in its own block on the taken path only, merge through
  a stack slot (arche's memory-backed value model, so no phi). Not a workaround
  ‚ÄĒ it's the canonical lowering. Verified truth values, nested chains, and that
  a reachable !abort still aborts.

  Test fixups (your calls)

  - zero_never_aborts.arche ‚Üí repurposed to divide-!zero (the surviving zero),
  adding the missing divide no-abort coverage.
  - pool_column.arche ‚Üí dropped the two now-invalid bounds-!zero lines.

  Status: clean -Werror build, 566/566 tests, 630 files round-trip, web-server
  builds. Decisions D13‚ÄďD15 recorded in docs/POLICY_MACROS.md.

  One thing to flag: to fix the harness write collision I also made the
  duplicate-decl check skip any pair involving a DECL_ORIGIN_STDLIB decl (stdlib
  symbols are module-namespaced and never duplicate a global/core/user name).
  With the switch to dprintf that guard is no longer strictly needed, but it's a
  correct invariant and future-proofs core symbol additions ‚ÄĒ let me know if
  you'd rather I revert it to keep the diff minimal.

  Next up per your earlier note: refactor the benchmarks and check for
  regressions ‚ÄĒ say the word.

‚óŹ Verified ‚ÄĒ yes, it's the same bounds machinery. emit_index_policy
  (codegen.c:4610) is the single unified path for all three index kinds, and
  they converge on identical final lines (4649-4657):

  i32idx = trunc <index> to i32
  emit_policy_inline(ctx, cg_policy_for(ctx, policy, 1), /*category*/1, {len,
  idx}, ...)
  clamped = sext out to i64

  Same (len, i) macro, same bounds category (1), same inliner. The only thing
  that differs is where len comes from:

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź

  The policy itself (clamp/abort/wrap/undefined) never knows which ‚ÄĒ it just
  receives (len, i). Semantic validation is uniform too: all three call
  bnd_policy_check ‚Üí validate_explicit_policy(..., POLICY_CAT_BOUNDS).

  Two nuances worth flagging:

  1. Pool len is the live count, not capacity. So on a pool an index policy
  bounds against how many entities are currently alive, whereas on a T[N] array
  it bounds against the full declared size. Same mechanism, but len means
  something different ‚ÄĒ a clamp on a pool clamps into the live region, which is
  usually what you want but is a semantic distinction, not just an
  implementation one.
  2. Slice construction (a[x:y]) is bounds too, but applied differently ‚ÄĒ that's
  a separate call site (the slice codegen, not emit_index_policy). It runs the
  same bounds policy on both endpoints (lo, hi) against N+1 boundary positions
  (the fencepost model, D10). Slice element indexing (s[i]) goes through
  emit_index_policy exactly like arrays.

  So: one bounds category, one macro, one inliner ‚ÄĒ the kinds differ only in how
  the length operand is computed.

‚óŹ Both areas are now clear. Key findings:

  Slices ‚ÄĒ the hidden hi = max(hi, lo) select (codegen.c:1924-1927) silently
  turns any reversed slice into an empty one, regardless of policy. So a[3:1] 
  !abort (both bounds in range, but reversed) silently makes an empty slice
  instead of aborting, and !undefined still gets the guard instead of being raw.
  There's a clean principled fix: the reversed/negative-length case is just the
  policy applied a third time ‚ÄĒ to the length hi - lo against [0, N] ‚ÄĒ so
  abort‚Üíaborts on reversed, clamp‚Üíempty, undefined‚Üíraw. No name-special-casing,
  no hidden select.

  Pools ‚ÄĒ POLICY_CAT_POOL exists in the type system but is completely unwired:
  the HIR call/insert node has no policy field, the parser doesn't accept
  insert(P,x) !name, and codegen never reads a pool policy. Today insert is
  hardcoded: full pool ‚Üí ok=0 (total), stale-handle delete ‚Üí abort. So adding
  user control means wiring the category through
  HIR‚Üíparser‚Üílower‚Üísemantic‚Üícodegen plus core pool policies.

  Let me confirm the design intent before writing the plan:

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ For the hidden slice behavior: the fix is to apply the slice's *own* 
     policy to the length `hi-lo` (not a silent `max`). This changes behavior ‚ÄĒ 
     a reversed-but-in-range slice like `a[3:1] !abort` would now ABORT (today 
     it silently makes an empty slice), `!clamp` gives empty, `!undefined` gives
     a raw negative length. Is that the behavior you want? ‚Üí Yes ‚ÄĒ policy 
     decides

     support.) ‚Üí reject (ok=0), abort, undefined (raw), does the signature 
     change depending on if there is a policy or not
     ¬∑ What should an UNANNOTATED `insert(P,x)(h:,ok:)` (no `!policy`) do on a

     ¬∑ Scope for this work ‚ÄĒ both, or sequence them? ‚Üí Both now

‚óŹ Both areas are clear, and the answers resolve the design ‚ÄĒ including your
  sub-question about the out-list. Let me record the answer to that: yes, the 
  signature changes with the policy, and it falls out cleanly:

  - !reject (and the default) can soft-fail ‚Üí insert(P,x) !reject (h:, ok:) ‚ÄĒ ok
  mandatory.
  - !abort aborts on full, !undefined always writes ‚Üí neither can return ok=0 ‚Üí
  insert(P,x) !abort (h:) ‚ÄĒ just the handle.

  And the pool policy fits the macro model with operands (count, cap, ok) where
  ok starts at 1: reject sets ok=0 when full, abort calls _exit, undefined is
  empty (‚Üí raw write, the capacity check is the policy's job, so opting out
  removes it). Let me write the plan.

‚óŹ Updated plan
  ‚éŅ ¬†/plan to preview

‚óŹ The plan is written. Here's the summary for your approval:

  Part A ‚ÄĒ Slice (surgical, one function): Replace the hidden hi = max(hi, lo)
  net-clamp in codegen_slice with a third application of the slice's own policy
  ‚ÄĒ to the length hi - lo against [0, N]. So abort aborts on a reversed slice,
  clamp gives empty, undefined gives a raw negative length. No
  name-special-casing, no hidden select. (Leaving the documented escape hatches
  alone: the -1 foreign-pointer trust path and provably-safe omitted bounds.)

  Part B ‚ÄĒ Pools (full pipeline): Wire @policy(pool) for insert, with the macro
  operands (count, cap, ok) (ok init 1; codegen writes iff ok stays 1):
  - Core policies: reject (if count>=cap: ok=0 ‚ÄĒ the default), abort (_exit +
  "arche: pool full"), undefined (empty ‚Üí raw write, capacity check opted out).
  - Out-list arity follows the policy: reject ‚Üí (h:, ok:) mandatory;
  abort/undefined ‚Üí (h:), ok forbidden.
  - Changes: policy field on the HIR call node, parser accepts insert(P,x) !name
  (h:,ok:), lower threads it, semantic validates category + arity + default,
  codegen inlines the pool policy in @arche_insert_<arch>.

  Both get tests + doc decisions (D16 slice-reversed, D17 pool category).

  One thing I decided rather than asked, since it follows directly from the
  macro model ‚ÄĒ the capacity check lives inside the reject/abort policy bodies
  (not codegen), so undefined opting out of it is what makes it a raw write.
  That keeps "nothing built in" intact. It's in the plan; flag it if you'd
  rather the check be codegen-owned.

‚óŹ Here's the concrete code ‚ÄĒ both the new core policy decls and how they're used
  at call sites.

  New pool policies in core/core.arche

  // ===== pool (insert) policies =====
  // Inlined at `insert(P, x)`. Operands: `count` = live entities, `cap` =
  capacity, `ok` = soft-fail
  // flag (starts 1). After the policy runs, the slot is written iff `ok` is
  still 1, else the handle
  // is 0. The capacity check lives HERE, not in codegen ‚ÄĒ so `undefined`
  (empty) opts out of it and
  // writes raw, exactly like `!undefined` on a bounds op.

  @policy(pool) reject :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int) {
    if (count >= cap) { ok = 0; }          // full -> report ok=0, no write (the
  default, total)
  }

  @policy(pool) abort :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int) {
    if (count >= cap) {
      dprintf(2, "arche: pool full\n")(_:);
      _exit(134);
    }
  }

  @policy(pool) undefined :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int) {
  }                                        // empty -> ok stays 1 -> raw write
  (overflows a full pool)

  These sit alongside the existing @policy(bounds) and @policy(divide) blocks.
  Note abort and undefined now exist in three categories (bounds, divide, pool)
  ‚ÄĒ the op site's category picks which, same as today.

  How they read at the call site

  P :: arche { v :: int }
  P[8]

  // default (no policy) == !reject ‚ÄĒ ok is mandatory, signals a full pool:
  insert(P, 10)(h:, ok:);
  if (ok == false) { /* pool was full */ }

  // explicit reject, same shape:
  insert(P, 10) !reject (h:, ok:);

  // abort: crashes on a full pool, so ok can never be 0 -> no ok in the
  out-list:
  insert(P, 10) !abort (h:);

  // undefined: always writes (raw) -> also no ok:
  insert(P, 10) !undefined (h:);

  The out-list arity is the visible consequence of the policy: reject can
  soft-fail so you must capture ok; abort/undefined can't, so the handle alone
  is the form (a stray ok: there is an error).

  Slices ‚ÄĒ the policy decls don't change, only the hidden codegen does

  The bounds policies (clamp/abort/wrap/undefined) stay exactly as they are.
  What changes is that the slice codegen stops hiding the max(hi,lo) net-clamp
  and instead applies the same policy to the length. So the existing
  !clamp/!abort/!undefined simply start governing the reversed case too:

  a: int[4];
  b := a[3:1] !abort;      // reversed & in-range -> NOW aborts (today: silently
  empty)
  c := a[3:1] !clamp;      // -> empty slice (len 0)   (clamp owns this, not the
  compiler)
  d := a[3:1] !undefined;  // -> raw negative length (UB), consistent with
  !undefined elsewhere

  Mechanically that's one extra emit_policy_inline(pol, {N+1, hi-lo}) replacing
  the icmp sge+select ‚ÄĒ the length is bounded into [0,N] by whatever policy is
  on the slice.

  Want me to adjust anything ‚ÄĒ the operand names (count/cap/ok), the "arche: 
  pool full" text, or the insert(P,x) !abort (h:) syntax placement ‚ÄĒ before I
  re-submit the plan?

‚úĽ Worked for 10m 49s

‚ĚĮ shouldn't policies be for bugs and not expected errors?                       
                                                                                
  Or should we even care about that distinction? is this what we should do      
  either way?                                                                   

‚óŹ That's the sharpest question in this whole design, and I think you've found a
  real flaw in my plan. Let me reason it out.

  The distinction is real, and it matters. A failure policy answers "what 
  happens when an operand is outside the valid domain?" ‚ÄĒ and the reason that
  question needs a policy is that the operation has no other channel to report
  it. a[i], a[lo:hi], a/b each return a single value; there's no room for an "it
  failed" flag, so the only ways to handle an out-of-domain input are: crash
  (abort), define a total result (clamp/wrap/zero), or UB (undefined). The
  policy is that decision.

  insert is not like that. It already returns its failure as a first-class
  value: (h:, ok:). A full pool isn't a bug ‚ÄĒ you declared P[8] knowing it can
  fill; ok=0 is the designed, expected outcome you branch on, exactly like
  malloc returning null or a bounded queue rejecting a push. It's already total,
  by construction. Bolting @policy(pool) onto it dresses up ordinary control
  flow as an exceptional condition.

  And look at what the three "pool policies" actually collapse to under that
  lens:
  - reject (ok=0) ‚ÄĒ that's just the existing return-value contract, not a
  policy.
  - abort on full ‚ÄĒ that's an assertion ("I sized this so it never fills"), not
  a recovery.
  - undefined (skip the check, write raw) ‚ÄĒ only sensible if full is impossible‚Ä¶
  i.e. another assertion, but one that corrupts memory on a condition you know
  can happen.

  So all three are either "the channel you already have" or "an assertion." None
  of them is the thing policies exist for. Pools don't need a policy.

  Notably, delete already embodies the right split without a policy: a stale
  handle (a use-after-free bug, no sensible value to return) ‚Üí abort;
  generation-exhausted (an expected resource limit) ‚Üí ok=0. Bug ‚Üí crash,
  expected ‚Üí value. That's the principle, and it's already there.

  So my honest recommendation flips: drop Part B. Keep insert(P,x)(h:, ok:)
  as-is ‚ÄĒ it's already the principled handling of an expected error. Part A (the
  slice fix) stays regardless, because that's not adding a policy ‚ÄĒ it's
  removing hidden behavior from one that already exists, and a reversed slice is
  a genuine domain violation with no failure channel.

  The one thing we'd lose is the "assert this pool never fills" sugar ‚ÄĒ but
  that's just if (ok == false) { ... } in user code today, and a real assertion
  mechanism would be a separate feature, not a misfiled policy.

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ Given that a full pool is an expected error already handled by the `(h:, 
     ok:)` return channel (whereas index/slice/divide have no channel and 
     genuinely need a policy), how should we treat pools? ‚Üí I like policies, 
     because I want the ability to write custom overflow logic in an idiomatic 
     way. But I don't like that it changes the signature (confusing) and maybe 
     it should be visually different from "panic" policies, should still be some
     sort of policy
     ¬∑ Should we also write down this principle as a design decision (so the 
     bugs-vs-channel distinction guides future ops)? ‚Üí Yes, document it

‚óŹ I think you've pinpointed exactly the right shape: pools should have policies
  (so overflow handling is custom and idiomatic), but they're a different kind
  of policy from the bug/panic ones ‚ÄĒ they handle an expected condition and
  return through the (h:, ok:) channel, they don't crash. So: keep the policy
  mechanism, keep the signature stable (always (h:, ok:)), and make them
  visually distinct from ! panic policies.

  Let me ground the syntax options before proposing. Checking what sigils are
  free and how the policy postfix parses:

‚óŹ Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche

‚óŹ ? is free in the lexer (!, :, | are taken; ? and likely ~ are not). So a
  distinct sigil is feasible. Here's the refined shape:

  - Panic policies (@policy(bounds|divide), used with !) ‚ÄĒ handle a domain
  violation on a channel-less op: crash / total-result / UB.
  - Handler policies (@policy(pool), used with a different sigil) ‚ÄĒ run custom
  logic on an expected condition, returning through (h:, ok:). Never change the
  signature; the out-list is always (h:, ok:).

  And the sigil-must-match-the-kind becomes a real check: a[i] ?clamp and
  insert(P,x) !evict are both errors, which reinforces the distinction
  semantically rather than just cosmetically.

  The one thing genuinely worth your eye is which sigil:

  ‚éŅ ¬†Done (14 tool uses ¬∑ 53.9k tokens ¬∑ 2m 41s)
  (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ The subagent came back with a strong, evidence-backed answer ‚ÄĒ and it found a
  genuine bug in the process. Synthesizing:

  The clincher (a latent soundness bug): A handle is gen<<32 | slot, generation
  starts at 0, so the first inserted entity gets handle 0 ‚ÄĒ and the full-pool
  path also writes h=0 as its failure value. So the failure sentinel already
  collides with a valid handle; ok is the only thing disambiguating them today.
  That's a bug to fix regardless of this decision.

  The data: of 176 insert call sites, exactly 2 branch on ok; ~158 discard both
  outputs with (_:, _:). Handles are never dereferenced for data (they're
  lifetime tokens, gen-checked only at delete‚Üíabort). So ok carries one bit,
  almost never read.

  The consensus: the slot-map/ECS world arche's pools resemble (slotmap, EnTT,
  generational-arena) and Rust Option/Zig error-unions all use one channel with 
  a reserved invalid sentinel, never a side ok bool.

  Recommendation (subagent's, and I agree): drop ok; signal failure with a
  reserved null handle (e.g. all-ones, which no real slot<cap<2¬≥¬≤ can equal);
  default handler ?reject (implicit, overridable ‚ÄĒ not mandatory, mirroring how
  !abort is implicit for bounds); and add a must-use-style lint so a fallible
  handle can't silently flow into delete unchecked. This answers your instinct
  precisely ‚ÄĒ the handle is the result, so a separate ok is redundant, and it
  fixes the collision instead of papering over it.

  The one caveat: an in-band failure is the C-malloc-null footgun without the
  must-use lint, so that lint is part of the deal, not optional.

  This is a bigger change than the original pool-handler plan (a ~176-site
  codemod, mostly mechanical, plus the sentinel + lint + null-at-delete
  handling), so it's your call on scope. The slice fix (Part A) is independent
  and proceeds either way.

     a reserved null handle. Which direction? ‚Üí what would a "null handle" even
     be? null == 0 pretty much in arche
     ¬∑ If we drop `ok`: how strict should the must-use safety net be (so an 
     in-band failure can't be silently ignored like a forgotten malloc 
     null-check)? ‚Üí will decide after we figure out what we are doing with ok

‚óŹ Sharp objection ‚ÄĒ that's the real issue. The subagent proposed an all-ones
  (-1) sentinel precisely because 0 is a valid handle today (slot 0, gen 0). But
  you're right that -1 is unidiomatic when null conventionally reads as 0.
  There's a cleaner answer (start generations at 1, so handle 0 becomes a
  collision-free null), but let me have the subagent verify it against arche's
  actual null convention rather than me hand-waving.

‚óŹ Resolved. Here's the concrete answer to "what would a null handle even be":

  arche has no null keyword ‚ÄĒ 0 is the conventional null, and a handle is just 
  an i64. The reason 0 can't serve as null today is the bug: the handle is (gen 
  << 32) | slot and generations start at 0, so the first entity (slot 0, gen 0)
  gets handle 0.

  The fix is to start generations at 1. Then every live handle has gen ‚Č• 1 ‚Üí
  high 32 bits nonzero ‚Üí handle ‚Č• 2¬≥¬≤, so a valid handle is never 0. That
  reserves 0 as the null handle ‚ÄĒ exactly arche's existing 0-is-null convention,
  no weird -1 sentinel. Mechanically: initialise gen_counters to 1 instead of
  zero-init (codegen.c:4128), delete still increments, the stale-check is
  unchanged (it compares stored vs. handle gen, which stays consistent). delete
  of a reinserted slot still aborts as before.

  Crucially, this fix stands on its own ‚ÄĒ even if we keep (h:, ok:), the
  overflow path's h=0 becomes an unambiguous "failed" value instead of colliding
  with a real first handle. So it's a foundational correctness fix either way.

  With null == 0 now clean and idiomatic, dropping ok reads naturally:
  h := insert(P, x) ?reject;
  if (h == 0) { /* pool was full */ }
  That's ordinary arche (0-is-null), one channel, the handle is the result. So
  my recommendation stands and is actually stronger now: drop ok (with gen-at-1
  making 0 the null), keep ?reject as the implicit default, and we'll settle the
  must-use lint next.

  One thing I'd verify during implementation: whether comparing a handle to 0 is
  currently allowed (handles may be a distinct type with no arithmetic) ‚ÄĒ the
  "handles are null-checked" language in the diagnostics suggests it's intended,
  but it may need a small rule to permit h == 0.

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ Now that a null handle is well-defined (`0`, made collision-free by 
     starting generations at 1), what's the call on `ok`? ‚Üí let's consider some 
     real scenarios. would there ever be a situation that we want to handle an 
     ok instead of letting a policy do it?

‚óŹ Good ‚ÄĒ let's reason from concrete cases. The question is really: is there a 
  failure response the caller needs that a policy can't give? Here are the real
  scenarios:

  1. Fire-and-forget (particle spawn, full ‚Üí just drop it). The reaction is
  generic and local. A ?reject policy handles it completely; the caller doesn't
  even look at the result. ‚Üí policy, no branch.

  2. Propagate the failure up the call stack (router: trie/table full ‚Üí return
  503 to the HTTP layer). A policy runs in place ‚ÄĒ it can reject/evict/abort,
  but it cannot return from your function. So the caller must branch and
  propagate. ‚Üí caller branches.

  3. Fall back to a different resource (PoolA full ‚Üí try PoolB; or recycle a
  specific entity for a reason only this site knows). The fallback is call-site
  logic a generic policy can't encode. ‚Üí caller branches.

  4. Conditional / contextual abort ("I sized this exactly; a failure here is a
  logic bug, crash with my context"). ‚Üí caller branches (if (h==0) { ‚Ä¶ }).

  So yes ‚ÄĒ scenarios 2‚Äď4 genuinely need the caller to handle failure, not a
  policy. But notice every one of them branches on the same single bit: "did I 
  get a usable entity?" And that bit is exactly h == 0:

  h := insert(TrieNode, key) ?reject;
  if (h == 0) { return ...503... }      // propagate

  h := insert(PoolA, x) ?reject;
  if (h == 0) { h = insert(PoolB, x) ?reject; }   // fallback

  The reason this works is that insert has exactly one soft-failure mode ‚ÄĒ full.
  (The subagent confirmed: generation-exhaustion is a delete concern; there's
  no "valid handle but failed" state and no second failure reason.) So a
  separate ok bool carries the same one bit as h == 0. It's redundant. And if
  insert ever did grow multiple failure reasons, a bool couldn't distinguish
  them anyway ‚ÄĒ you'd want an error code, not ok.

  The only thing ok could add that the handle can't is a "succeeded, but you 
  should know something happened" signal ‚ÄĒ e.g. an ?evict handler that made room
  by kicking someone out. But that's not "ok/fail"; it's a side-channel
  notification, and it doesn't exist in the v1 set (reject only). If we ever
  want it, it belongs in the handler (log/signal), not in every call's
  signature.

  So the answer to your question: yes, callers must sometimes handle failure
  themselves rather than delegate to a policy ‚ÄĒ but they do it by checking the
  returned handle (h == 0), which is strictly more useful than a bool and
  idiomatic once gen-at-1 makes 0 a clean null. The two mechanisms aren't
  competitors: the policy is the reusable local reaction (drop/evict/grow); the 
  handle-check is the site-specific follow-up 
  (propagate/fallback/abort-with-context). ok is the redundant third wheel.

  My recommendation is therefore unchanged and now well-grounded: drop ok, 
  branch on h == 0.

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ Given that every real caller-handles-it scenario branches on the single 
     bit `h == 0` (insert has one failure mode), does that settle dropping `ok`?
     ‚Üí how does h==0 if h is not an int?

‚óŹ Another real type question. The handle isn't a bare int ‚ÄĒ it's a distinct
  handle type (you can't do arithmetic on it or read fields through it). So h ==
  0 needs the type system to permit comparing a handle to the null literal. Let
  me check what arche already supports here, because there's a hint it does:

‚óŹ Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche
      echo "=== codegen: handle/opaque 'non-null cell test' ==="; sed -n
      '647,660p' codegen/codegen.c‚Ä¶)
  ‚éŅ ¬†=== codegen: handle/opaque 'non-null cell test' ===
     static const char *cond_int_type(const HirExpr *cond) {
        if (!cond)
     ‚Ä¶ +18 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ A handle is a distinct type (no arithmetic, no field reads through it) ‚ÄĒ but
  it's i64-backed, and arche already has the machinery for this: cond_int_type
  (codegen.c:647-650) has a dedicated "non-null cell test" path that treats a
  handle (and an opaque cell) as i64 when it's used as a condition. So
  mechanically if (h) / if (h == 0) already lowers to an i64 compare against 0 ‚ÄĒ
  the null test is half-built.

  What it needs is a small, targeted semantic rule: a handle may be compared to
  the literal 0 (its null sentinel) and to another handle (identity) ‚ÄĒ and
  nothing else (no +, no h.field, unchanged). That's exactly the shape of a
  nullable-pointer / optional null-check: the token has one distinguished "none"
  value you may test for, but it's still not an integer you can do math on. So:

  h := insert(P, x) ?reject;
  if (h == 0) { ... }     // null-check: compare the token to its null sentinel
  // or the truthiness form, which the cell-test path already supports:
  if (h) { /* got one */ }

  So h == 0 isn't "treating a handle as an int" ‚ÄĒ it's "testing the handle
  against null", which happens to be 0 because that's arche's null and gen-at-1
  reserves it. Small rule, already-present codegen, no blocker.

  That clears the last feasibility concern, so drop-ok is the direction (handle
  is the result; if (h == 0) is the check; policies handle the generic local
  reactions). The one piece you explicitly deferred ‚ÄĒ the safety net so an
  in-band failure can't be silently ignored like a forgotten malloc null-check ‚ÄĒ
  is now the thing to settle:

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ With `ok` dropped (failure = null handle `0`), how strict should the 
     safety net be so a failed insert can't be silently used? ‚Üí lets just keep 
     ok? or use err codes or something. It not a boolean, booleans don't even 
     exist so that concern isn't valid

‚óŹ You're right on both counts, and the booleans point is the one that actually
  changes the math: arche has no bool, so ok is an int ‚ÄĒ which means it's not a
  redundant 0/1 flag, it's a status channel that can carry an error code. A null
  handle can only ever say "got one / didn't" (one bit); an int ok can
  distinguish reasons and extend to future failure modes. That's a real
  capability a null handle can't match, and it undercuts my "redundant" framing.
  So: keep ok.

  That also makes the whole must-use/null-sentinel apparatus unnecessary ‚ÄĒ ok is
  the explicit channel, and the existing discarded_ok lint already nudges you
  to read it. The detour was worth it (it surfaced a genuine latent bug ‚ÄĒ see
  below), but we land back at the cleaner place: keep ok, add ? handlers to
  customize the overflow response.

  So the reconciled principle is sharper than my first cut:
  - No failure channel (index/slice/divide) ‚Üí a panic policy (!) defines the
  outcome: crash / total-result / UB.
  - Has a failure channel (insert's ok) ‚Üí a handler policy (?) customizes what 
  happens on the expected condition (reject/evict/grow/log); the channel still
  reports it. The handler doesn't invent crash behavior ‚ÄĒ it shapes the normal
  result.

  And the capacity check lives in the handler (reject = if (count>=cap) { ok = 
  0; }), so an empty handler naturally means "no check, raw write" ‚ÄĒ the pool
  analog of !undefined falls out for free, available if someone writes it, not
  shipped.

  The one keeper from the detour: the h=0 collision is a real latent bug (first
  entity gets handle 0; overflow also writes 0 ‚ÄĒ only ok disambiguates them).
  Since we're keeping ok it's disambiguated, not dangerous, so the
  gen-start-at-1 fix becomes optional hardening (makes a handle independently
  null-checkable) rather than required. I'll note it as a small recommended
  cleanup, not core.

  Let me fold all this into the plan.
‚óŹ Updated plan
  ‚éŅ ¬†/plan to preview

‚ĚĮ question: does the current policy system let you do something like: 
                                                                      
  policy:                                                             
  i = oldest(FooArche.timestamp)                                      
  (new insert now replaces the entity at i)                           

‚óŹ Great question ‚ÄĒ it cuts to whether handlers are actually worth having. Honest
  answer: no, not as the system stands today, and not in the v1 I just wrote.
  Two things are missing.

  A policy body today only gets scalar operands and can only mutate them. My
  planned pool handler is policy(count, cap, ok) ‚ÄĒ it can decide ok, but it has
  no way to (a) read the pool's columns to find the oldest, or (b) choose which 
  slot the insert writes to. The write slot is picked by the compiler (free-list
  head, else bump count); the handler can't redirect it. So i = 
  oldest(Foo.timestamp); <write into i> isn't expressible.

  What it would take is a real but bounded extension ‚ÄĒ and it composes
  beautifully with the generation model:

  // `slot` = the write target (defaults to the next free/bump slot); the
  handler may redirect it.
  @policy(pool) evict_oldest :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int, slot: int)
  {
    if (count >= cap) {
      oldest_i := 0;
      for (j := 1; j < count; j = j + 1) {
        if (Foo.timestamp[j] < Foo.timestamp[oldest_i]) { oldest_i = j; }
      }
      slot = oldest_i;          // recycle the oldest slot instead of failing
    }
  }
  insert(Foo, x) ?evict_oldest (h:, ok:);

  Three pieces beyond v1:
  1. A slot operand the handler can set (the write target), not just ok.
  2. Column reads inside the handler body (Foo.timestamp[j]). The inliner
  already codegens body statements through the normal expression path, so a scan
  loop + column read is plausibly already supported mechanically ‚ÄĒ it's just
  never been exercised; oldest(...) would be that loop (or a future stdlib
  reduction), not a builtin.
  3. Codegen writing into slot with a generation bump. This is the elegant part:
  overwriting slot i bumps gen[i], so the evicted entity's old handle
  automatically goes stale ‚ÄĒ any later use of it aborts, exactly like a normal
  delete. Eviction correctness falls out of the generation system for free.

  So it's very much in the spirit of the model, just past where v1 stops. And
  it's arguably the reason to have handlers at all ‚ÄĒ reject alone is barely more
  than the default, but eviction/recycling (LRU caches, ring buffers, bounded
  entity pools) is real custom overflow logic.

  One caveat to design through: your example names Foo concretely, so the
  handler is tied to one archetype. That's fine (you write the eviction policy
  for your pool), but it means either the handler is monomorphic per-archetype
  (semantic checks the named pool matches the insert target) or we add a generic
  self-pool binding later. v1-with-eviction would do the simple monomorphic
  version.

  This reshapes Part B, so your call on scope:

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ Should pool handlers be powerful enough to evict (scan a column, pick a 
     victim slot, redirect the write) ‚ÄĒ your `i = oldest(Foo.timestamp)` example
     ‚ÄĒ or ship reject-only first? ‚Üí Reconsider the whole approach

‚óŹ You picked "reconsider" ‚ÄĒ good, because the eviction example exposes that I
  may have been forcing a square peg. Let me think about this from the ground
  up, but first check one thing that determines which approaches are even
  viable: whether arche can write logic that's generic over a pool/archetype
  (vs. only per-archetype). That's the hinge.

‚óŹ Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche
      echo "=== systems: how do they operate over archetypes? ==="; grep -rn
      "system\|run " tests/unit/language/systems/*.arche 2>/dev/null |‚Ä¶)
  ‚éŅ ¬†=== systems: how do they operate over archetypes? ===
     tests/unit/language/systems/module_body_run.arche:1:// Regression: a `
     run <sys>` inside a proc in an IMPORTED module (a non-entry compilatio
     ‚Ä¶ +47 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Investigating the codebase reframes the whole thing. The decisive fact: arche 
  has no generics over archetypes ‚ÄĒ all pool logic is written against a 
  concrete, named archetype. The router (the real stdlib pool user) names
  TrieNode.first_child[cur], TrieNode.kind[child] etc. everywhere; systems run
  over named archetypes; there's no proc(P: archetype). So a "policy" buys you
  almost nothing over a normal proc for pools ‚ÄĒ both are per-archetype-concrete.
  The only thing the ?handler annotation adds is auto-invoking-on-overflow.
  That's sugar, not capability.

  Which means I have to question the premise I talked us into. Walk back through
  it:

  - Your original need was "let the user decide overflow behavior, and know when
  it happens." Both of those are already satisfied ‚ÄĒ ok tells you it happened,
  and ordinary code decides what to do. No policy required.
  - Eviction ‚ÄĒ your i = oldest(Foo.timestamp) example ‚ÄĒ is just ordinary arche 
  code: scan a column (a plain loop, exactly like router does), pick a victim,
  recycle it, retry. It names Foo concretely because all arche code names
  archetypes concretely. A "policy" doesn't make it more reusable or more
  powerful; it just hides it behind an annotation and forces it through a
  constrained macro that can't even express the scan.
  - The macro-operand handler (count, cap, ok, slot) was me trying to cram a
  real decision procedure into the tiny operand-mutation shape that fits
  clamp/zero. It doesn't fit. That mismatch is the awkwardness you kept sensing.

  So my honest reconsidered position: pools should not have a policy mechanism 
  at all. Policies earn their place exactly where there's no failure channel
  (index/slice/divide ‚ÄĒ you can't write if around a value that doesn't exist).
  Pools have a channel; overflow is expected; handling it is ordinary
  application logic, written the way arche writes everything else.

  What that leaves:
  - insert(P,x)(h:, ok:) unchanged ‚ÄĒ keep ok, no ?, no @policy(pool), no new
  sigil. The branch-on-ok is the mechanism.
  - Eviction is user code. The one thing worth adding is a small data-structure 
  primitive so eviction is ergonomic ‚ÄĒ e.g. a way to recycle/delete a slot you
  picked by index (today delete needs a handle), or you store handle<P> and
  delete that. That's a pool primitive, not a policy.
  - Part A (slice) still stands ‚ÄĒ it's about removing hidden behavior from a
  genuine (channel-less) policy, unrelated to all this.

  This is less machinery, more honest, and matches how arche already works. The
  cost is that a reusable overflow behavior is "write a proc that names your
  pool" rather than "attach a named policy" ‚ÄĒ but in a language with no
  archetype generics, those are the same thing anyway, and the proc is the more
  capable of the two.

‚óŹ¬†User answered Claude's questions:
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ Given arche is per-archetype-concrete (no generics) and insert already 
     has the `ok` channel, which direction for pool overflow? ‚Üí I definitely 
     want the option to have a basic way to handle most cases. Sometimes you 
     just want to write a generic handler and have it apply everywhere.
     ¬∑ If we drop the policy: add a primitive to make eviction ergonomic, or 
     leave it to existing primitives? ‚Üí we'll see

‚óŹ That settles it ‚ÄĒ and your "generic, apply everywhere" point is exactly the
  piece I undervalued. Here's the correction to my own reasoning:

  I said "a policy buys nothing over a proc" because both are per-archetype. But
  that's wrong for a generic handler. A proc can't be written once and applied
  to every pool ‚ÄĒ arche has no archetype generics. A policy can, precisely
  because it's an inlined macro that the compiler specializes at each insert
  site against that site's concrete archetype. So the handler mechanism is
  arche's only way to write "one overflow rule, applied everywhere" ‚ÄĒ which is
  exactly what you want. That's the real justification I was missing, and it
  means handlers stay in.

  So the reconsideration wasn't wasted ‚ÄĒ it pinned down why they earn their
  place (generic reuse a language-without-generics otherwise can't express) and
  where the line is:

  - Generic handlers touch only pool-agnostic facts ‚ÄĒ count, cap, ok. Written
  once, apply to any pool: ?reject (the default, applied everywhere with no
  annotation), ?reject_at_90pct, ?log_then_reject. This is the macro-operand
  model, and it's genuinely generic. ‚úÖ ships.
  - Column-scan eviction (oldest(Foo.timestamp)) is not generic ‚ÄĒ it names a
  concrete column, so it's pool-specific and needs a slot operand + column
  access (+ a self/designated-column mechanism to ever be generic). That's the
  "we'll see" extension ‚ÄĒ deferred, not precluded.

  So the settled design: keep ? handler policies for their generic reuse; keep
  ok (the channel); stable (h:, ok:); operands (count, cap, ok) (pool-agnostic ‚áí
  generic); capacity check lives in the handler (empty body ‚áí raw); default
  reject applied everywhere. Eviction deferred. Part A (slice) unchanged. Let me
  lock this into the plan.

‚óŹ Updated plan

‚óŹ The design is settled and the plan is updated. Calling for approval.

     ‚Ēā runtime range exits 0.                                                  ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā ---                                                                     ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā Part B ‚ÄĒ Pools: generic HANDLER policies (@policy(pool), sigil ?)       ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā Two kinds of policy, enforced                                           ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā - Panic ‚ÄĒ @policy(bounds|divide), invoked expr !name. (Unchanged.)      ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā - Handler ‚ÄĒ @policy(pool), invoked insert(P,x) ?name (h:, ok:).         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā The use-site sigil must match the resolved policy's kind; a mismatch is ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā a compile error: a[i] ?clamp                                            ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā and insert(P,x) !reject both error. Distinction is semantic, not        ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā cosmetic.                                                               ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā Model ‚ÄĒ generic, signature always (h:, ok:)                             ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā A handler is inlined at insert with pool-agnostic operands (count, cap, ‚Ēā

     ‚Ēā once, specialized per site.                                             ‚Ēā

     ‚Ēā else h=0; the out-list                                                  ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā is always (h:, ok:) (no per-policy arity). The capacity check lives in  ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā the handler, so an                                                      ‚Ēā

     ‚Ēā free. Default (unannotated insert)                                      ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā resolves to reject, applied everywhere ‚ÄĒ identical to today's behavior. ‚Ēā
     ‚Ēā                                                                         ‚Ēā

     ‚ēį‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ēĮ

‚óŹ Good ‚ÄĒ let's design the pool-specific case concretely, since that's the
  compelling one. The key realization: a pool-specific handler is just ordinary 
  arche code that names the pool's columns ‚ÄĒ exactly how every proc in arche
  already works (the router names TrieNode.kind[child] throughout). The only new
  ingredient is a slot operand: the write target the handler can redirect to
  evict.

  So the unified model ‚ÄĒ every @policy(pool) handler gets operands (count, cap, 
  ok, slot):
  - count, cap ‚ÄĒ occupancy (read)
  - ok ‚ÄĒ status, init 1; set 0 to reject
  - slot ‚ÄĒ the write target, init to the next free/bump slot; redirect it to 
  evict

  and genericity is emergent: a handler that touches only operands is generic
  (any pool); one that names a concrete pool's columns is specialized to that
  pool.

  // GENERIC ‚ÄĒ applies to every pool (the default)
  @policy(pool) reject :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int, slot: int) {
    if (count >= cap) { ok = 0; }
  }

  // POOL-SPECIFIC ‚ÄĒ names Foo's columns, so it's specialized to Foo
  @policy(pool) evict_oldest :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int, slot: int)
  {
    if (count >= cap) {
      oldest := 0;
      for (j := 1; j < count; j = j + 1) {
        if (Foo.timestamp[j] < Foo.timestamp[oldest]) { oldest = j; }
      }
      slot = oldest;        // recycle the oldest slot
    }
  }
  insert(Foo, x) ?evict_oldest (h:, ok:);

  How it runs: inline the handler over (count, cap, ok=1, slot=<next slot>).
  Then: ok==0 ‚Üí reject (h=0); else write the new entity into slot. If slot is an
  already-occupied slot (eviction), codegen bumps gen[slot] first, so the
  evicted entity's old handle goes stale automatically ‚ÄĒ any later use of it
  aborts, identical to a delete. The new handle comes back fresh. Eviction
  correctness falls straight out of the generation system.

  Semantics: a handler that names Foo's columns is restricted to insert(Foo, ‚Ä¶)
  (using it on another pool is a compile error) ‚ÄĒ it's specialized, not generic.
  Caveats to design through honestly: the scan assumes live slots are
  0..count-1 (true for overwrite/ring pools; a pool that also deletes leaves
  holes the scan would need to skip), and "oldest" is whatever column logic you
  write.

  The one real design fork is where the pool-specific handler lives ‚ÄĒ named in
  its body (per-call), or owned by the pool so all its inserts use it:

‚óŹ¬†User declined to answer questions
  ‚éŅ ¬†¬∑ How should a pool-specific handler bind to its pool? (Name the pool in 
     the body (per-call ?) / Pool owns its overflow policy / Both)

‚ĚĮ the POOL can own the policy, sure. But not the archetype, and don't conflate  
  them.                                                                         

‚óŹ You're right ‚ÄĒ I conflated two different things. The archetype Foo :: arche { 
  timestamp :: int; ‚Ä¶ } is the schema (what an entity is). The pool Foo[8] is
  the storage (how many slots exist). Overflow is purely a storage concern ‚ÄĒ
  it's about capacity, which the schema knows nothing about ‚ÄĒ so the policy
  belongs on the pool declaration, never the archetype:

  Foo :: arche {          // archetype = schema. No policy here.
    timestamp :: int
    payload   :: int
  }

  Foo[8] ?evict_oldest    // pool = storage (cap 8) + its overflow handler.
  Policy lives HERE.

  @policy(pool) evict_oldest :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int, slot: int)
  {
    if (count >= cap) {
      oldest := 0;
      for (j := 1; j < count; j = j + 1) {
        if (Foo.timestamp[j] < Foo.timestamp[oldest]) { oldest = j; }
      }
      slot = oldest;
    }
  }

  insert(Foo, x)(h:, ok:);   // uses the pool's handler; reject if Foo[8]
  declared none

  So: the handler attaches at Foo[N] ?name; a pool with no ? defaults to reject;
  the schema stays clean. That keeps storage policy with storage.

  One thing left to pin down: whether a call site can override the pool's
  handler.

  Q: Should insert be able to override per-call (insert(Foo, x) ?reject (h:,ok:)
  even though Foo[8] declared ?evict_oldest), or is the overflow handler owned
  solely by the pool declaration (one policy per pool, set at Foo[N], no
  per-call form)?

‚úĽ Cogitated for 57s

‚ĚĮ sure you can  override per call, but we might need a lint to warn that insert 
  Foo ?policy body has references to a different archetype or something like    
  that                                                                          

‚óŹ That's the complete picture. Settling it:

  - Archetype (schema) ‚Č† pool (storage). Policy attaches at the pool decl Foo[N]
  ?handler, never the archetype.
  - Per-call override allowed: insert(Foo, x) ?reject (h:, ok:) overrides the
  pool's declared handler. Resolution: per-call ?name ‚Äļ pool's ?name ‚Äļ reject
  default.
  - Lint (warn, not error): an insert(Foo, ‚Ä¶) ?h whose handler body references a
  different archetype's columns (Bar.timestamp) is probably a mismatch ‚ÄĒ flag
  it, but don't forbid it.
  - Plus everything prior: ? sigil, operands (count, cap, ok, slot), slot
  redirect ‚Üí eviction with gen-bump-makes-old-handle-stale, keep ok, stable (h:,
  ok:).

  Let me lock the whole thing into the plan.

  pool-owned handlers with per-call override, the (count, cap, ok, slot) model
  with eviction via slot-redirect + gen-bump, and the cross-archetype lint.

‚óŹ User approved Claude's plan
  ‚éŅ ¬†Plan saved to: ~/.claude/plans/pull-up-last-session-whimsical-teacup.md ¬∑ 
     /plan to edit
     Plan: un-hide slice behavior + pool overflow handlers (? policies)

     Context

     The failure-policy macro model covers bounds (index/slice/pool-column) and
     divide. Two gaps violate the
     project principle ‚ÄĒ policies own correctness; nothing hidden, nothing built
     in ‚ÄĒ and the second
     clarified what policies are for, and the archetype-vs-pool distinction.

     Principle. A policy answers "what happens when an operand/condition leaves 
     the valid domain?" Its
     shape depends on whether the op has a failure channel:
     - No channel ‚ÄĒ a[i], a[lo:hi], a/b return one value ‚Üí crash / total-result
     / UB. Panic
     policies: @policy(bounds|divide), invoked expr !name.
     - Has a channel ‚ÄĒ insert(P,x)(h:, ok:) reports a full pool via ok (an int;
     arche has no bool,
     so it can grow into an error code). Overflow is expected. arche has no 
     generics over archetypes
     (all pool code names a concrete archetype, e.g. the router's
     TrieNode.kind[child]), so a handler
     policy ‚ÄĒ a macro the compiler specializes per insert site ‚ÄĒ is arche's only
     way to write a
     generic overflow rule applied to every pool. Handler policies:
     @policy(pool), invoked
     insert(P,x) ?name (h:, ok:), or declared on the pool.

     Archetype ‚Č† pool. Foo :: arche { ‚Ä¶ } is the schema (what an entity is);
     Foo[N] is the pool
     (storage, capacity N). Overflow is a storage concern, so the policy
     attaches to the pool decl
     Foo[N] ?handler, never the archetype. delete already splits bug-vs-expected
     with no policy: stale
     handle (use-after-free bug) ‚Üí abort; generation-exhausted (expected) ‚Üí
     ok=0.

     ---
     Part A ‚ÄĒ Slice: replace the hidden max(hi,lo) with a third policy 
     application

     In codegen_slice (codegen/codegen.c:1902-1934), after the panic policy runs
     on lo and hi (each
     into [0,N] via len = N+1 = np1), replace the icmp sge + select net-clamp
     (:1924-1927) with the
     same policy applied to the length hi - lo:

     rawlen32 = trunc (hi_c - lo_c)                              ; may be
     negative (reversed)
     len_out  = emit_policy_inline(pol, /*bounds*/1, {np1, rawlen32})   ; bound
     length into [0,N]
     len2     = sext len_out ; ptr2 = base + lo_c

     clamp‚Üíreversed length clamps to 0 (empty); abort‚Üíaborts when hi-lo<0 so
     a[3:1] !abort now
     aborts (was silently empty); undefined‚Üíraw negative length. OOB bounds
     still caught on the lo/hi
     application; the length application is a no-op for in-range slices. Leave
     documented (not hidden):
     cap = base_cap - lo (negative only under !undefined); omitted bounds
     default to safe lo=0/hi=N;
     the base_len == -1 foreign-pointer trust path.

     Files: codegen/codegen.c (codegen_slice).
     Tests: reversed slice per policy ‚ÄĒ a[3:1] !abort‚Üí134, !clamp‚Üíempty,
     !undefined‚Üíraw builds;
     a no_abort case (reversed runtime range, !clamp, exit 0).

     ---
     Part B ‚ÄĒ Pool overflow handlers (@policy(pool), sigil ?)

     Two policy kinds, enforced

     - Panic ‚ÄĒ @policy(bounds|divide), expr !name. (Unchanged.)
     - Handler ‚ÄĒ @policy(pool), insert(P,x) ?name (h:, ok:) and/or declared on
     the pool.

     Sigil must match kind: a[i] ?clamp and insert(P,x) !reject both error.

     Model

     A handler inlines at insert with operands (count, cap, ok, slot):
     - count = live entities (high-water ‚ąí free_count), cap = capacity
     (read-only).
     - ok = status, init 1; set 0 to reject (‚Üí h=0, no write).
     - slot = write target, init to the next free/bump slot; the handler may
     redirect it to evict.

     Codegen inlines the handler, then: ok==0 ‚Üí h=0; else write the new entity
     into slot. If slot is
     an already-occupied slot (eviction), bump gen[slot] first ‚ÄĒ the evicted
     entity's old handle goes
     stale automatically (later use aborts, like delete); the fresh handle is
     returned. count is
     unchanged on eviction (replace, not add). Out-list is always (h:, ok:); the
     capacity check lives
     in the handler (empty body ‚áí raw write).

     Genericity is emergent. A handler that touches only operands is generic ‚ÄĒ
     applies to any pool:
     @policy(pool) reject :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int, slot: int) {
       if (count >= cap) { ok = 0; }          // the default, applies everywhere
     }
     A handler that names a concrete pool's columns is pool-specific (eviction):
     @policy(pool) evict_oldest :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int, slot:
     int) {
       if (count >= cap) {
         oldest := 0;
         for (j := 1; j < count; j = j + 1) {
           if (Foo.timestamp[j] < Foo.timestamp[oldest]) { oldest = j; }
         }
         slot = oldest;                       // recycle the oldest -> eviction
       }
     }

     Where it binds + resolution

     - Pool owns its handler: Foo[8] ?evict_oldest. Every insert(Foo,‚Ä¶) uses it.
     - Per-call override: insert(Foo, x) ?reject (h:, ok:) overrides for that
     site.
     - Resolution precedence: per-call ?name ‚Äļ pool-decl ?name ‚Äļ reject default.
     - Lint (warn, not error): an insert(Foo,‚Ä¶) whose resolved handler body
     references a different
     archetype's columns (Bar.timestamp) is a probable mismatch ‚ÄĒ flag it; don't
     forbid (a cross-pool
     evict is unusual but legal).

     Caveats to design through

     - The scan idiom assumes live slots are 0..count-1 (true for overwrite/ring
     pools); a pool that also
     deletes leaves holes the user's scan would need to skip. Document, don't
     enforce.
     - "Oldest" is whatever column logic the user writes ‚ÄĒ no oldest() builtin
     in v1 (it's a plain loop).

     Pipeline changes

     - lexer/lexer.{c,h} ‚ÄĒ ? ‚Üí TOK_QUESTION.
     - hir/hir.h ‚ÄĒ char *policy; (+ sigil-marker bit) on the call expr
     (HirExpr.call); a policy
     field on the pool/static-archetype decl node.
     - parser/parser.c ‚ÄĒ accept ?name postfix on (a) the insert call (between
     call and out-list) and

          +taxView v, SourceLoc loc) {                                   
      563 +  SyntaxView pol = sv_child(v, SN_POLICY_REF);

‚óŹ Now let me add the core reject handler so ?reject resolves, and verify B2
  validation passes for the happy path. Operand model: handlers declare the
  prefix of (count, cap, ok, slot) they use ‚ÄĒ reject needs only the first three:

‚óŹ Update(core/core.arche)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Added 12 lines
       96  }
       97  @policy(divide) undefined :: policy(a: int, b: int) {
       98  }
       99 +                                                              
      100 +// Pool overflow HANDLER policies ‚ÄĒ category `pool`, invoked w
          +ith `?` (`insert(P,x) ?reject`), not `!`.                     
      101 +// A full pool is an EXPECTED condition (reported via `ok`), n
          +ot a bug, so a handler shapes the result                      
      102 +// rather than crashing. Operands are the prefix of `(count, c
          +ap, ok, slot)` the handler uses: `count` =                    
      103 +// live entities, `cap` = capacity (read-only); `ok` starts 1 
          +(the insert writes iff it stays nonzero);                     
      104 +// `slot` is the write target (declare it to redirect the writ
          +e ‚ÄĒ eviction). The capacity check lives                       
      105 +// HERE, so an empty handler is a raw write. `reject` is the d
          +efault applied to every pool.                                 
      106 +@policy(pool) reject :: policy(count: int, cap: int, ok: int) 
          +{                                                             
      107 +  if (count >= cap) {                                         
      108 +    ok = 0;                                                   
      109 +  }                                                           
      110 +}

‚óŹ Update(docs/POLICY_MACROS.md)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Added 32 lines
      102    policy prelude it carries ‚ÄĒ in its own block entered only on
            the taken path, merging the result through
      103    a stack slot (arche's value model is memory-backed, so no ph
           i). Not a workaround: it *is* the canonical
      104    short-circuit lowering. Covered by `policy/midexpr/{and,or,d
           ivide_and}_shortcircuit.arche`.
      105 +- **D16. Slice reversed = the policy applied to the length.** 
          +The slice codegen used to emit a hidden                       
      106 +  `hi = max(hi, lo)` net-clamp so the length couldn't go negat
          +ive ‚ÄĒ invisible to the policy, so                             
      107 +  `a[3:1] !abort` (reversed but in-range) silently became an e
          +mpty slice and `!undefined` got the guard                     
      108 +  anyway. Removed: after applying the policy to `lo` and `hi` 
          +(each into `[0,N]`), the policy is applied                    
      109 +  a **third time to the length `hi - lo`** against `[0,N]`. So
          + `clamp`‚Üíempty, `abort`‚Üíaborts on a                           
      110 +  reversed slice, `undefined`‚Üíraw negative length ‚ÄĒ all visibl
          +e policy decisions, no special-casing.                        
      111 +  The last compiler-owned net-clamp is gone. (`policy/slice_re
          +versed_{abort,clamp}.arche`.)                                 
      112 +- **D17. The response shape follows the failure channel; that'
          +s what a policy is *for*.** A policy                          
      113 +  answers "what happens when an operand/condition leaves the v
          +alid domain?" ‚ÄĒ and the shape depends on                      
      114 +  whether the op has a way to *report* it. **No channel** (`a[
          +i]`, `a[lo:hi]`, `a/b` return one value) ‚Üí                    
      115 +  a **panic policy** (`@policy(bounds|divide)`, sigil `!`): cr
          +ash / total-result / UB. **Has a channel**                    
      116 +  (`insert(P,x)(h:, ok:)` reports a full pool via `ok`, an `in
          +t`) ‚Üí a **handler policy**                                    
      117 +  (`@policy(pool)`, sigil `?`) shapes the *result*; the channe
          +l reports it, no crash. Don't police what a                   
      118 +  return value already reports: `delete` already splits this w
          +ith no policy (stale handle = use-after-free                  
      119 +  *bug* ‚Üí abort; generation-exhausted = expected ‚Üí `ok=0`). `o
          +k` stays an `int` and can widen to an error                   
      120 +  code later without a signature change.                      
      121 +- **D18. Pool overflow handlers (`?`): generic OR pool-specifi
          +c, owned by the *pool* (storage), not the                     
      122 +  *archetype* (schema).** `Foo :: arche {‚Ä¶}` is the schema; `F
          +oo[N]` is the pool; the handler attaches at                   
      123 +  `Foo[N] ?handler` because overflow is a storage concern. A h
          +andler is a macro the compiler specializes                    
      124 +  per insert site ‚ÄĒ arche's only way to write a *generic* over
          +flow rule (one rule, any pool), since it has                  
      125 +  no archetype generics. Operands `(count, cap, ok, slot)` (th
          +e prefix a handler declares): `ok` init 1                     
      126 +  (the insert writes iff it stays nonzero), `slot` the write t
          +arget (declare it to **redirect** ‚Üí an                        
      127 +  eviction overwrites that slot; codegen bumps its generation 
          +so the evicted entity's handle goes stale,                    
      128 +  for free). A handler touching only operands is generic (`rej
          +ect`, the default); one naming a pool's                       
      129 +  columns is pool-specific (`evict_oldest` scans `Foo.ts`). Th
          +e capacity check lives in the handler (empty                  
      130 +  body = raw write). Resolution: per-call `?name` ‚Äļ pool-decl 
          +`?name` ‚Äļ `reject`. Sigil must match kind                     
      131 +  (`a[i] ?clamp` / `insert !reject` error). A handler reading 
          +a *different* archetype's columns than the                    
      132 +  insert target is W0019 (lint). Codegen: `reject` is the help
          +er's built-in ok=0-on-full (zero overhead,                    
      133 +  passes `evict_slot = -1`); any other handler is inlined at t
          +he call site to compute `evict_slot`, which                   
      134 +  the shared `@arche_insert_<arch>` helper overwrites (reusing
          + its write/gen-bump blocks).                                  
      135 +  (`policy/pool_{reject,evict,evict_stale,override,cross_arch_
          +lint}.arche`,                                                 
      136 +  `policy/errors/{handler_sigil_mismatch,insert_panic_sigil,po
          +ol_wrong_category}.arche`.)

‚óŹ Update(docs/POLICY_MACROS.md)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Added 9 lines, removed 3 lines
       8  ## The model
       9  
      10  A `policy` is a **macro**. The compiler inlines its body at a f
          allible op, with the op's operands
      11 -bound as **mutable locals**, then runs the **raw** op on whatev
         -er they now are:                                               
      11 +bound as **mutable locals**, then runs the **raw** op on whatev
         +er they now are. There are two kinds,                          
      12 +split by whether the op has a way to *report* failure (see D17)
         +:                                                              
      13  
      13 -- bounds index/slice ‚Üí operands `len, i` ; the policy mutates `
         -i` (or terminates), then `base[i]` raw.                        
      14 -- divide ‚Üí operands `a, b` ; the policy mutates them, then `a /
         - b` raw.                                                       
      14 +- **panic policies** (`@policy(bounds|divide)`, invoked `expr !
         +name`) ‚ÄĒ for channel-less ops:                                 
      15 +  - bounds index/slice ‚Üí operands `len, i` ; the policy mutates
         + `i` (or terminates), then `base[i]` raw.                      
      16 +  - divide ‚Üí operands `a, b` ; the policy mutates them, then `a
         + / b` raw.                                                     
      17 +- **handler policies** (`@policy(pool)`, invoked `insert(P,x) ?
         +name (h:, ok:)`, or declared on the pool                       
      18 +  `Foo[N] ?name`) ‚ÄĒ for a pool insert, whose full-pool conditio
         +n is an *expected* result reported via                         
      19 +  `ok`. Operands `count, cap, ok, slot` (the prefix the handler
         + declares); the insert writes iff `ok`                         
      20 +  stays nonzero, into `slot` (redirect it to evict). See D18.  
      21  
      22  Consequences that "fall out" of the model (no special-casing):
      23  - **`undefined`** = a policy with an **empty body**: no mutatio
          n ‚áí the raw op. Not a keyword.

‚óŹ Everything is green. Let me mark the final task complete and give a summary.

‚óŹ Both parts of the approved plan are fully implemented and green.

  What landed

  Part A ‚ÄĒ slice hidden behavior removed. The silent hi = max(hi, lo) net-clamp
  in codegen_slice is gone; the slice policy is now applied a third time to the
  length hi ‚ąí lo. So reversed/negative-length is a visible policy decision:
  a[3:1] !abort aborts (was silently empty), !clamp ‚Üí empty, !undefined ‚Üí raw.
  The last compiler-owned net-clamp is gone.

  Part B ‚ÄĒ pool overflow handlers (?), the second policy kind.
  - Archetype ‚Č† pool: the handler attaches to the pool decl Foo[N] ?handler
  (storage), never the schema.
  - Two kinds, enforced: ! panic (bounds/divide, channel-less) vs ? handler
  (pool, has the ok channel). Mismatched sigil is a compile error; wrong
  category is E0124; unknown is rejected.
  - Generic or pool-specific via emergent genericity: reject (operands only, the
  default applied everywhere) vs evict_oldest (names Foo.ts, scans a column).
  - Eviction works: operands (count, cap, ok, slot); redirect slot to a victim
  and the new entity overwrites it. The victim's generation is bumped, so its
  old handle goes stale (use-after-evict aborts, verified) ‚ÄĒ eviction
  correctness falls out of the generation system for free.
  - Resolution: per-call ?name ‚Äļ pool-decl ?name ‚Äļ reject. Per-call override
  verified.
  - W0019 lint: a handler reading a different archetype's columns than the
  insert target.
  - You also got the formatter normalization you asked for (!clamp/?reject print
  tight, not ! clamp).

  Codegen approach (no hacks): reject is exactly the helper's built-in
  ok=0-on-full ‚Üí zero overhead, passes evict_slot = -1. Any other handler is
  inlined at the call site (where normal codegen ‚ÄĒ loops, column reads ‚ÄĒ works)
  to compute evict_slot, which the shared @arche_insert_<arch> helper overwrites
  by reusing its existing write/gen-bump blocks. No monomorphization, no
  duplicated slot logic.

  Verification

  - make all clean (-Werror), 580/580 tests, 644 files round-trip, web-server
  builds clean.
  - 10 new committed tests (slice reversed √ó2, pool
  reject/evict/evict-stale/override/cross-arch, sigil/category errors √ó3);
  decisions D16‚ÄďD18 recorded in docs/POLICY_MACROS.md.

  Nothing was retired, no known-failures added, no workarounds. The one
  explicitly-optional item I skipped is the gen_counters-start-at-1 hardening ‚ÄĒ
  with ok kept, the h=0/overflow collision is already disambiguated by ok, so it
  was hygiene-only, not needed.

  All changes are on disk and unstaged ‚ÄĒ per your standing rule I haven't
  touched git.
